widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=118349757 id=\"118349757\" class=\"abstract-title\" >ABSTRACT</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We investigate the effect of microlensing on the standardization of strongly lensed Type Ia supernovae (GLSNe Ia). We present predictions for the amount of scatter induced by microlensing across a range of plausible strong lens macromodels. We find that lensed images in regions of low convergence, shear and stellar density are standardizable, where the microlensing scatter is \u2272 0.15 mag, comparable to the intrinsic dispersion of a typical SN Ia. These standardizable configurations correspond to asymmetric lenses with an image located far outside the Einstein radius of the lens. Symmetric and small Einstein radius lenses (\u2272 0.5 arcsec) are not standardizable. We apply our model to the recently discovered GLSN Ia iPTF16geu and find that the large discrepancy between the observed flux and the macromodel predictions from More et\u00a0al. cannot be explained by microlensing alone. Using the mock GLSNe Ia catalogue of Goldstein et\u00a0al., we predict that \u223c22\u00a0per\u2009cent of GLSNe Ia discovered by Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST) will be standardizable, with a median Einstein radius of 0.9 arcsec and a median time delay of 41\u00a0d. By breaking the mass-sheet degeneracy the full LSST GLSNe Ia sample will be able to detect systematics in <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> at the 0.5\u00a0per\u2009cent level.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: micro&quot;\">gravitational lensing: micro</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;supernovae: general&quot;\">supernovae: general</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;supernovae: individual: iPTF16geu&quot;\">supernovae: individual: iPTF16geu</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmological parameters&quot;\">cosmological parameters</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmology: observations&quot;\">cosmology: observations</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=118349759 id=\"118349759\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The value of the Hubble constant <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> is a major point of contention in cosmology today, bringing the validity of the Lambda-Cold Dark Matter (\u039bCDM) model of cosmology into question. This particularly arises from the 3.4\u00a0\u03c3 tension between the Planck Collaboration et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) result of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a067.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.9\u2009km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, derived from the cosmic microwave background (CMB), and the Riess et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) result of <em>H</em><sub>0\u00a0</sub>=\u00a073.2\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.7\u2009km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, measured from low-redshift supernovae and Cepheids. The Planck Collaboration et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) result infers <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> assuming \u039bCDM, whilst the Riess et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) result probes <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> directly. Whilst this tension could be attributed to statistical fluke or unaccounted systematics, it potentially signals new physics beyond the \u039bCDM model. Hence the need for precise and independent measurements of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> is greater than ever, such that the validity of the \u039bCDM model can be tested.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Strong gravitational lenses are powerful probes of cosmology (Oguri et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Suyu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Collett &amp; Auger <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) and are particularly sensitive to <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> through time delay cosmography (Treu &amp; Marshall <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). The light from each image in a lensing system takes a different path through the lens before reaching the observer. If the lensed object is a variable source, the images vary asynchronously with a geometrical time delay based on these path differences. Time delays have an additional component caused by the gravitational potential of the lens (Shapiro <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1964</a>). When a background source peaks sharply in luminosity, the time delay between each image can in principle be measured by observing the time difference between the peaks of each image.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Time delays allow strong gravitational lenses to measure <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> independently of assumptions made in the cosmological model: the value of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> is mostly invariant to other cosmological parameters such as the curvature and the dark energy equation of state (Bonvin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). The most recent example is Bonvin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), which used time delays from three lensed quasars to independently measure <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$H_0 = 71.9^{+2.4}_{-3.0}$|</span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> to within a 3.8\u00a0per\u2009cent precision. In order to obtain a value for <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> through strong lens time delays, one needs to know the 2D lens potential and the unlensed source position, neither of which can be observed directly. The use of lens modelling is therefore required in order to infer these quantities.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">However, strong lens models are subject to degeneracies, which are a major source of uncertainty for time delay cosmography (Schneider &amp; Sluse <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). The main component of the degeneracy is the mass-sheet degeneracy: when rescaling the mass of the lens with an additional sheet of mass of constant density, the image configurations remain exactly the same but the projected mass on each image (also known as the convergence \u03ba) changes, affecting the time delay (Falco, Gorenstein &amp; Shapiro <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1985</a>). Put simply, two lens models producing identical image configurations can have very different time delays. Breaking the mass-sheet degeneracy is therefore necessary to constrain <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. In order to break the mass-sheet degeneracy additional information is required, such as the intrinsic luminosity of the background source (Kolatt &amp; Bartelmann <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1998</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Originally proposed by Refsdal (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1964</a>), the prospect of using strongly lensed supernovae (GLSNe) to precisely measure <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> is promising, especially after the discovery of the Type Ia GLSN iPTF16geu in 2016 October (Goobar et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). The light curves of Type Ia Supernovae (SNe Ia) are standardizable (Phillips <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>), allowing us to infer their intrinsic luminosity. Hence, GLSNe Ia can potentially lift the mass-sheet degeneracy (Oguri &amp; Kawano <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>) and enable a test of systematic uncertainties in time delay cosmography.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">GLSNe are advantageous over the lensed active galactic nuclei (AGN) currently used for time delay cosmography (Vuissoz et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Suyu &amp; Halkola <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Tewes et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Bonvin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). SN light curves have a strong peak before they decay, occurring over a time-scale of several weeks, whilst AGN light curves vary stochastically and heterogeneously, with weak variations in luminosity. Hence GLSN time delays can be obtained in a single observing season, whilst AGN must be monitored over several years in order to acquire accurate time delays (Liao et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Goldstein et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>) predicts that \u223c930 GLSNe Ia will be discovered by the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST) (LSST Science Collaboration et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) over its 10 yr survey, with 70\u00a0per\u2009cent of the GLSNe Ia having time delays that can be measured precisely.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Despite the potential power of GLSNe Ia, there exists one major theoretical barrier to their use as cosmological probes: microlensing caused by stars in the foreground lensing galaxy. Microlensing can independently magnify or demagnify individual images of the background source (Bagherpour, Branch &amp; Kantowski <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Dobler &amp; Keeton <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>), introducing scatter into the shape and amplitude of the resulting light curves. The effect of microlensing on each lensed image can be inferred by obtaining its convergence \u03ba,<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">1</a></sup> shear \u03b3, and smooth matter fraction <em>s</em> through lens modelling. \u03ba and \u03b3 represents the amount of mass projected on and near the image, respectively, while <em>s</em> represents the projected fraction of mass in dark matter as opposed to stellar matter (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). Due to the distribution and random motion of the stars in the foreground galaxy, inferring the effect of microlensing on one image does not infer the effect of microlensing on the other image(s). This can significantly reduce the reliability of any time delay and luminosity measurement, as microlensing can randomly distort the light curve of each image, such that the intrinsic magnification and luminosity of the source can be difficult to determine. This effect also evolves over time. As the background SN Ia grows, the number of microlensing caustics that its light profile intersects with increases with time (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>). Recently, Goldstein et al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>) have shown that time delays can be robustly measured using early time colourcurves.</p>                        <a id=\"118349768\" scrollto-destination=\"118349768\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=WrE06kZVaNl1b0fOETHBk9jPI2hz5BAiul1ROYRSjGR9k44sWkeBTxLv7R-3ohJvbUU7vWQpDKYEe32iUkekOppxqRZzdbzfjP0ip5QZbYdewWI1maaC7G~JboEHLd2Yec6-6QdfP2oxWRBGk~gyHwzp-2xLl~HxsZRRU4jkkRn6rbgaVY732X5jf2-PHVQnQB~nZH2oa9aEfZUi2SVbib-5Z0CLwO07QZERiLh3Pvy-GFGN8zCxflnl80xzSv4sR8BTn0B0XrPfPmhJOigLQuRtgnSkt6dH8YNS0qKaFAOGkuKgaeMH7cJHCLKNkRjPWDkM5eaBz793RKQ4zlGgVg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Example of microlensing maps corresponding to various combinations of the convergence \u03ba, shear \u03b3, and smooth (dark) matter fraction s. At each time-step, the SN Ia profile is convolved with the microlensing background: the magnifications inside the disc are summed up and averaged. As the SN Ia profile grows, it crosses more and caustics, causing the microlensing magnification to vary over time. The resulting magnifications are shown in the bottom panel.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">More et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) modelled iPTF16geu using the GLAFIC (Oguri <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and GLEE (Suyu &amp; Halkola <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Suyu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) macro lens models. Whilst the models themselves were in agreement, they were in contention with the Goobar et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) observations, with a discrepancy of almost 2\u00a0mag for the brightest image. Their conclusion was that the disparity between their lens models and the observations was primarily due to microlensing from foreground stars in the lensing galaxy.<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">2</a></sup></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we examine the effect microlensing has on an expanding SN Ia profile across a wide range of image configurations corresponding to particular values of \u03ba, \u03b3, and <em>s</em>. We provide the first predictions for regions of parameter space where the SN Ia image has a standardizable light curve, allowing us to infer its intrinsic luminosity and hence break the mass-sheet degeneracy. We define a standardizable supernova as one where the scatter due to microlensing is less than 0.15 mag, comparable to the intrinsic dispersion of an unlensed SN Ia after standardization (Betoule et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Macaulay et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). We also present predictions for the fraction of GLSNe Ia discovered by LSST that will be standardizable. Finally we analyse the effect of microlensing on iPTF16geu and compare our results against the More et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) prediction. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a> we describe our microlensing simulations. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a> we present and discuss our subsequent analysis with results and conclude in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a>. Throughout this paper we report results in the observer time frame assuming a source redshift of 0.409.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=118349772 id=\"118349772\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 SIMULATIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To simulate the effect of microlensing by stars in the lens galaxy, we use magnification maps generated by the GERLUMPH project<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">3</a></sup> as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> (Vernardos et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Vernardos &amp; Fluke <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). These are pixelated maps of the source plane where the magnification per pixel has been calculated using the inverse ray-shooting technique (Kayser, Refsdal &amp; Stabell <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1986</a>). A field of randomly distributed point masses is used to simulate the star field, with each star having the same mass. The deflections for each microlens are computed directly and in parallel using the graphics processing unit (GPU) implementation of Thompson et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Each magnification map used in this work is square with 10\u00a0000 pixels on a side, with a side corresponding to 13.7 <em>R</em><sub>Ein, \u2299</sub>. <em>R</em><sub>Ein, \u2299</sub> is the Einstein radius for a 1 M<sub>\u2299</sub> microlens; for a lens at <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z$|</span><sub><em>l</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.216 and a source at <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z$|</span><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.409. This corresponds to a physical scale of <em>R</em><sub>Ein, \u2299</sub> = 4 \u00d7 10<sup>11</sup> km = 2 \u00d7 10<sup>\u22126</sup> arcsec on the sky, with each map pixel covering an area of 2.5 \u00d7 10<sup>17</sup> km<sup>2</sup> = 9 \u00d7 10<sup>\u221218</sup> arcsec<sup>2</sup> on the sky.<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn4\" data-open=\"fn4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">4</a></sup></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To sample a wide range of possible GLSN configurations we use the GD1 set of maps (described in detail in Vernardos et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). This set covers \u03ba, \u03b3 space on a uniform grid with \u0394\u03ba, \u0394\u03b3\u00a0=\u00a00.05, and 0 \u2272 \u03ba, \u03b3 \u2272 1.7. For each \u03ba, \u03b3 combination there are 11 values of <em>s</em> available: 0 \u2264 <em>s</em> \u2264 0.9, in steps of 0.1, and <em>s</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.99. For each \u03ba, \u03b3 pair we use maps with a smooth matter fraction <em>s</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8. This results in a total of 4488 magnification maps.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To obtain the SN microlensing light curves we convolve the magnification maps with a time varying profile of the background source (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>). We use an expanding uniform disc to approximate the SN brightness profile. This simple model is sufficient for our purposes since the observed luminosity of a microlensed source is mostly sensitive to the average size and largely independent of any specific shape of the source profile (Mortonson, Schechter &amp; Wambsganss <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>). We do not consider sources with clumpy profiles.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The supernova expansion rate is set to 10<sup>4</sup> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> = 2.5 \u00d7 10<sup>\u22128</sup><em>\u00a0R</em><sub>Ein, \u2299</sub> s<sup>\u22121</sup>. Since this is much larger than any of the velocities involved (i.e. the peculiar velocity of the lens and the source, the velocity of the observer, and the proper motions of the microlenses), we can approximate the microlensing map as time invariant, and the centroid of a supernova as constant; only the radius of the supernova changes with time. At this expansion rate we are able to place 10<sup>4</sup> SNe per magnification map without profile overlap within the first 60\u00a0d.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To obtain a light curve for an individual SN, we choose a position on the magnification map, and evaluate the product of its profile and the magnification map at each time-step. This is done for a total of 55 time-steps: 0 &lt; <em>t</em> &lt; 16\u00a0d with \u03b4<em>t</em> = 1\u00a0d, 16 &lt; <em>t</em> &lt; 60\u00a0d with \u03b4<em>t</em> =\u00a02\u00a0d, and 60 &lt; <em>t</em> &lt; 200\u00a0d with \u03b4<em>t</em> =\u00a07\u00a0d. A total of \u2248 250\u00a0000 convolutions between maps and profiles have been performed, requiring roughly 300 GPU h.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We normalize all of our unlensed source fluxes to unity at all times, such that our light curves depend only on the microlensing rather than intrinsic variations in the unlensed source. We show a range of examples of microlensing light curvesin Fig. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"118349780\" scrollto-destination=\"118349780\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=eg-KKpGJXZJRTGCZmP8LdDDxP9huZ~LbOPSqhK9UHJTTgpi-svMFv8qjSqkx6vl6-8HFMnoKysvaFBwO7oyszopSXsmTerxAEXW6-aIIGhR3C6SNI0CECJuqYcSiJmQsUiRU73jy8N-jk0t1emYSPlW8981cyPF8~Zx7xiZDa0DT8GSI5P6m3snwZorieE8pGgLcPJ83KhkBvuqNGw7Ws-BVq3UtM46r0VBIwyNffJPpVAyNakOv9OiENaiOENu6Nt~CGmncvzuo-6P0kJlbA-7VKA~LpqUxA9V1sZSRGVpRcNS6ZfAb-TNTfXvAmAPbXBNT4Pm6YZe0tUqABo7DeA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A selection of simulated microlensing light curves for an expanding uniform disc. The four panels correspond to different values of the convergence \u03ba, shear \u03b3, and smooth matter fraction <em>s</em>. Each light curve within a panel has the same macrolensing parameters but a different realization of the microlensing by stars.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=118349781 id=\"118349781\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>\r\n                    <h3 scrollto-destination=118349782 id=\"118349782\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>3.1 Microlensing scatter</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">For each \u03ba\u2013\u03b3 pair, time-step, and value of the smooth matter fraction <em>s</em>, we measured the scatter by taking half the difference of the 16th and 84th percentile of the resulting probability density function (PDF).<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn5\" data-open=\"fn5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">5</a></sup></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we show how the microlensing scatter \u03c3<sub>ML</sub> varies with \u03ba, \u03b3, and <em>s</em>, across a range of times <em>t</em>. We find that there is a region of parameter space where the light curves from GLSNe Ia are standardizable as the scatter due to microlensing is comparable to the typical intrinsic dispersion for a SN Ia after standardization (Betoule et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Macaulay et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Therefore, with the correct lensing configuration, it is possible to infer the unlensed magnitude of the source SN Ia. This will lift the mass-sheet degeneracy and allow us to acquire an accurate, precise, and independent measurement of the Hubble Constant <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>.</p>                        <a id=\"118349785\" scrollto-destination=\"118349785\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=B7lMJ2vo6ALWVznMKKV6e7j~CVcv4iOEadUKkc8NjEY-nPFV9xoBFwl4GcEUYGdEJtKxJ5Dz1NfVsjNyzBYNkTXit9RT4AdUPEzzwOoiZpBvLoVj0BIRykY~diOIUrKRlq~b8twZjJf1T7frREHkGZ2Mntay7MjN2Tlib3RovCzVTes0426O2aTQ1OZdVz-Qk8MX5-6BH9SvQZSl9dkS~sGEHWOBLEp17KYgXIzRgElpcirJm8UF0q8zUMK44MoBmFb0wvSo2P1CWcZ6et6OqWtj9ctp6H520e90fNaYHYaCfmuvQ60QqJNrAZPPRspyioKBIDY432l5WumAfEXlFw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The microlensing scatter in the observed luminosity of a lensed supernova image. Each subpanel shows the scatter as a function of the convergence \u03ba and shear \u03b3 at fixed smooth matter fraction s and time t after explosion. The smooth matter fraction increases from top to bottom and the time of observation increases from left to right. The white pixels along the diagonal correspond to regions of infinite magnification: lensed images do not form here.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig4.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/118349785/sty1346fig4.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig4.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"118349785\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/sty1346fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&Signature=xwnlMsCAltNH8PpB3CffaV5KFPGIUJd-wXnKIkjWiXCTTmdh7f9ZiHBSujKsnXmpzHS8GMo9DuBiL9-NTlqH7VshKtoYcYIT84A7jYs8VAno-zZ~XmsEGtOF9FkxOkdrvJ3Helk-PBWkVoFiYoJa5B7KR-bEMjEGQRv~pb5q~mE6qn1qwCtuNT7WJyONRcVVPxk3-qEZ8AADltOL-Xuzcyw~RtIxT2~3nUGHblSTs7eOTYsONzeTm2NYf0t0nLe0GDzT-jMg4SE0zOsYVQHC-PFM09FUFUHAbEtKz~7JPmcyjpfr8X4i4N8E9EID01iaBhe74eguaXhdX3rSS-Cr9Q__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=118349785&ar=5001437&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig4.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The microlensing scatter in the observed luminosity of a lensed supernova image. The smooth matter fraction increases from top to bottom and the time of observation increases from left to right. The white pixels along the diagonal correspond to regions of infinite magnification: lensed images do not form here.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=B7lMJ2vo6ALWVznMKKV6e7j~CVcv4iOEadUKkc8NjEY-nPFV9xoBFwl4GcEUYGdEJtKxJ5Dz1NfVsjNyzBYNkTXit9RT4AdUPEzzwOoiZpBvLoVj0BIRykY~diOIUrKRlq~b8twZjJf1T7frREHkGZ2Mntay7MjN2Tlib3RovCzVTes0426O2aTQ1OZdVz-Qk8MX5-6BH9SvQZSl9dkS~sGEHWOBLEp17KYgXIzRgElpcirJm8UF0q8zUMK44MoBmFb0wvSo2P1CWcZ6et6OqWtj9ctp6H520e90fNaYHYaCfmuvQ60QqJNrAZPPRspyioKBIDY432l5WumAfEXlFw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The microlensing scatter in the observed luminosity of a lensed supernova image. The white pixels along the diagonal correspond to regions of infinite magnification: lensed images do not form here.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig4.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/118349785/sty1346fig4.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig4.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"118349785\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/sty1346fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&Signature=xwnlMsCAltNH8PpB3CffaV5KFPGIUJd-wXnKIkjWiXCTTmdh7f9ZiHBSujKsnXmpzHS8GMo9DuBiL9-NTlqH7VshKtoYcYIT84A7jYs8VAno-zZ~XmsEGtOF9FkxOkdrvJ3Helk-PBWkVoFiYoJa5B7KR-bEMjEGQRv~pb5q~mE6qn1qwCtuNT7WJyONRcVVPxk3-qEZ8AADltOL-Xuzcyw~RtIxT2~3nUGHblSTs7eOTYsONzeTm2NYf0t0nLe0GDzT-jMg4SE0zOsYVQHC-PFM09FUFUHAbEtKz~7JPmcyjpfr8X4i4N8E9EID01iaBhe74eguaXhdX3rSS-Cr9Q__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=118349785&ar=5001437&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig4.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The microlensing scatter in the observed luminosity of a lensed supernova image. Each subpanel shows the scatter as a function of the convergence \u03ba and shear \u03b3 at fixed smooth matter fraction <em>s</em> and time <em>t</em> after explosion. The smooth matter fraction increases from top to bottom and the time of observation increases from left to right. The white pixels along the diagonal correspond to regions of infinite magnification: lensed images do not form here.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We find that the standardizable region corresponds to lensed images with low \u03ba and \u03b3, with the size of the standardizable region increasing with <em>s</em>, i.e. images forming in regions of lower stellar density are less susceptible to microlensing. Physically, this corresponds to a lens with an asymmetric image configuration, with at least one image located far outside the Einstein radius of the lens. This outermost image experiences the least amount of microlensing due to being far away from the high-stellar density region of the lensing galaxy and hence could be used to infer the unlensed magnitude of the background SN Ia.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">As highlighted in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>, the microlensing scatter in low \u03ba, \u03b3 regions increases over time, meaning early time measurements of the SN image fluxes are optimal for cosmography. This counter-intuitive result is because there are few caustics in these situations and a small source will typically fall in the smooth region between caustics (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). A small number of systems will be highly magnified but these are excluded by our choice to define width as half the 68\u00a0per\u2009cent confidence region. As the source expands it is more likely to cross a caustic, creating a larger spread of magnifications at late time. The scatter does not decrease at late times as the source is still too small to average over many caustics. However, in higher \u03ba, \u03b3 regions, the scatter decreases with time and increases with <em>s</em>. This behaviour is due to the increased density of microlensing caustics. As the source expands it averages over more caustics and the scatter shrinks, but this still does not reach a standardizable level even after 200\u00a0d.</p>                        <a id=\"118349788\" scrollto-destination=\"118349788\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=0KA8~AvlUKR3AQbTuNiAciTZP3mW4aUiAYTPLjOiHXfnx-QZIUZ4ogPaDnW4ALtYEsiqX67tP0WIyFJ80FuuW3ohII16b5ASE9Gn4MeZAWJyYir6N2Tpp2CRJFuvEXRQe4YHGBQuAHwNmDDWJvePIYxhzVq9xEdWC3tXwxpCUoRqoeczlOZBufl6HeO2MdqjUnljDiASovaUVoPmBxzYACMGpktC4XXKxtDeybRYISmpnuxilO~VZs-xz~DHg~Hjx1HNLyfmK7oF4~iiZcMfdSIjDXmUlQZYCsGR5IlzMPU9Ad77chlsHgqWLpuaYcvlj5hEguPrbTgSs8vW2brwDw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The microlensing induced scatter in the observed luminosity as a function of time. The different lines correspond to different values of the smooth matter fraction s.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig5.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/118349788/sty1346fig5.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig5.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"118349788\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/sty1346fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&Signature=O8j3AuQUjjmJYve4D2955JyJgXJ1gVamqjLmUAobIeZK6Ob7nqcyy2x4UuEq9s8ynntFfwbTGQwSuy6YqkD7od9xvEjVktY~hOMbKQxapziQL5J5PuSHt-Gb9V765rArsESg9NuVJr0Vej0KGBP0zdJWk-772XlmOm9q6PNvaa~in3uxSSALeNoB9cFWtIM70cs601d0plEqUqINtSUNaiRTH86Cbdqib9Pmx6JHrQBCDDjHD165O7q9TIYOJNgsloLGzFPN3H3OlBt2uWiCs66mzVjAEjem~-KgdEeTmyszZ5DHSrKr0fQOt0AdML54L1v8sJYo14KLIPd13SwqPA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=118349788&ar=5001437&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig5.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The microlensing induced scatter in the observed luminosity as a function of time. The different lines correspond to different values of the smooth matter fraction <em>s</em>.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=118349789 id=\"118349789\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>3.2 How many lensed supernovae are standardizable?</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To investigate the fraction of lensed supernovae that will be standardizable, we first use a simple lens model to relate lensed image position to the expected microlensing scatter. We assume a singular isothermal sphere (SIS) lens model and iPTF16geu redshifts of <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z$|</span><sub><em>l</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.216 and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z$|</span><sub><em>s</em></sub> =\u00a00.409 for the lens and source, respectively.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">For each point on the image plane, \u03ba and \u03b3 can be inferred from the macro lens model, however, the macro model is sensitive only to the total mass and not the partition between stars and dark matter. The smooth matter fraction is given by the fraction of the surface density not in stars <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"update165118_equ1\" content-id=\"update165118_equ1\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}s = 1 - \\frac{\\kappa _{*}}{\\kappa _\\mathrm{tot}}. \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(1)</span></div>The total mass distribution is modelled using an SIS lens profile <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ2\" content-id=\"equ2\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}\\kappa _\\mathrm{tot} = \\kappa _\\mathrm{SIS} = \\frac{R_{\\mathrm{Ein}}}{2r}, \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(2)</span></div>where <em>r</em> denotes a position in the image plane in polar coordinates. For the stellar component of equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-update165118_equ1\"></span><a href=\"#update165118_equ1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">1</a>) we assume a de Vaucouleurs profile <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ3\" content-id=\"equ3\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}\\kappa _* = \\kappa _\\mathrm{deV}(r) = Ae^{-k(r/R_\\mathrm{e})^{1/4}}, \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(3)</span></div>where <em>A</em> is a normalization constant, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub> is the effective radius of the lens and <em>k</em> = 7.669 (Dobler &amp; Keeton <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>). To calculate the normalization constant A, we match the dark matter fractions to those found in typical strong lensing ellipticals in the Sloan Lens ACS (SLACS) sample (Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ4\" content-id=\"equ4\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}A = (1 - f_\\mathrm{DM})\\frac{M_\\mathrm{tot}}{M_*}, \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(4)</span></div>where <em>M</em><sub>tot</sub> and <em>M</em><sub>*</sub> were inferred by integrating equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ2\"></span><a href=\"#equ2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>) and (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ3\"></span><a href=\"#equ3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a>) in polar coordinates from 0 to <em>R</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>/2. <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> is the total projected fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius of the galaxy. Our <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> is then matched to the fit derived in Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ5\" content-id=\"equ5\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}f_\\mathrm{DM} = a \\times \\log (\\sigma _{R_{e}/2}) + b, \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(5)</span></div>where <em>a</em> and <em>b</em> are fitting parameters that depend on the initial mass function (IMF) of the lens and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\sigma _{R_{e/2}}$|</span><sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn6\" data-open=\"fn6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">6</a></sup> is the velocity dispersion within half the effective radius of the lens (Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). Assuming a Salpeter IMF, <em>a</em>=\u00a00.80\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.44 and <em>b</em>=\u00a00.05\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.18 while for a Chabrier IMF, <em>a</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.46\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.22 and <em>b</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.40\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09 (Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>).</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Using our model for \u03ba, \u03b3, and <em>s</em> across the image plane and the results of Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3-1\"></span><a href=\"#sec3-1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3.1</a>, we determine the microlensing scatter as a function of GLSN image plane position. This is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a> , which shows that standardizable images form beyond the Einstein radius corresponding to an asymmetric configuration. More of the image plane is standardizable if the Einstein radius is large or if the IMF is Chabrier rather than Saltpeter. The Chabrier IMF has a lower mass-to-light ratio, so places a larger fraction of the total mass in dark matter whereas the Salpeter IMF places more mass in low-mass stars.</p>                        <a id=\"118349793\" scrollto-destination=\"118349793\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig6\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 6.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig6.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=nUkXo80OWH1RbRSLoBukANUZj2WlhEnjlUj~vo9U8eOafUAQY9Kz~S7aPN-CROGiQ3hjo83sUzOVAaS-Yb~26ooGFldP3O~rZseneIGAyv43UFj9GG2K~puKJAqQ07CX3XnIlkQF8X-P9wq8NIHFLsDb9ZNkDF6BwOMcNqiQngvZypCjOznLQ3zBmZ9WsePvOLqU~bCMhuvvr1xVvDwWSnn7oDK1M7Ai9nEeFskj369Genr63Fe1vnJSqus5oaNsreen5~2X4IQoG55XWDySuToJhffadmdZWGsj8dM~GUKqGKWDLy-qzBSPSedUqxloHEzEipg9P0Putr1QxpFjpw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The amount of microlensing scatter induced on any lensed image at any point on the image plane, assuming an SIS lens and an SN 30\u00a0d after explosion. (2010).\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig6.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/118349793/sty1346fig6.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig6.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"118349793\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/sty1346fig6.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&Signature=ezV~TEusF51FAvuy-UXZWnkfYNWqkpZag1tzTC0dvNjbAPG8SbTVs0E6-AujYQNJVnAC2GzioUkgMxSeFnmNRW49Z5rK~rVQ0vhp8b6GfLmsCCqHvlBw6urDYnNdEd2hiAQWlj2TO17D8jZVpSf-AEztTsXBX~03uA4phUYmoe1-fr~7H7RAAYiFcZdH1USjpN58VXk3wyjHugAd50AoGMi7xrVkNdylocju3CihL~DyBcvx8Rd45w8cZFmDnM-9z~xaz~Z93fxDR8A07i7Hd8Gjf6hGBfAwnIiLIuCGYCIb25SuhT~eAGvl7uGe9EHUkAa2LzyJY5y9XzF0cYUUJw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=118349793&ar=5001437&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig6.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The amount of microlensing scatter induced on any lensed image at any point on the image plane, assuming an SIS lens and an SN 30\u00a0d after explosion. The four panels show the effect of varying the Einstein Radii, <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>, and the IMFs. The black dashed line represents the outermost boundary for multiple imaging. The inner white circle corresponds to the critical curve and infinite magnification. The Salpeter and Chabrier IMFs correspond to the dark matter fractions derived in Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>).</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In order to infer how often standardizable images form, we must determine the fraction of the source plane that is standardizable. We solve the lens equation for a range of source positions and Einstein radii. For each source position we infer the microlensing scatter for all images formed. Fig. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a> shows the fraction of the source plane that is standardizable as a function of <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>, for an SN 30\u00a0d after explosion, assuming either a Salpeter or Chabrier IMF. For a Salpeter IMF \u223c 70\u00a0per\u2009cent of the source plane is standardizable provided <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> \u2273 1 arcsec on the sky. Decreasing <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> causes the standardizable fraction of the source plane to sharply decline to zero at <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> \u223c 0.5 arcsec. More of the source plane is standardizable if the IMF is Chabrier: lenses as small as <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> \u223c 0.4 arcsec can have a source plane which is 70\u00a0per\u2009cent standardizable, but sharply dropping to 0\u00a0per\u2009cent at <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> \u223c 0.2 arcsec.</p>                        <a id=\"118349795\" scrollto-destination=\"118349795\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig7\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 7.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig7.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=InsEoKiFaL78pA-VkmsgI~Z5ZzgYERCQ25~wI9l9MKOICdiT9HuS8u0dz35dXiwomcHAxruh2Ok5br19PSBK2xHwPnM2zyJaIMjVp9g0moEUCYpvRnghkweF53PKq0RcU50UhJyEfIE1LZlAxseN4anl7vXDtTrWTARLyUzHk~YGGuR9mLtu9eUIOc8w6bwyo5a5EDA2pYE49WMyQ7ctUCl2TaOsvDbcvTWhZ3G-nzomDkNN6-GcHe0dLV6eNzzf~3ligHTgrFjvjAPpJ6bLP2iiNSs8xeLqoWlxoz~id-cTp0HjS8vQojEwR1lIJG6CO1JqIb~OAPJsM-OowXhp7g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The percentage of the source plane that is standardizable as a function of the Einstein radius REin of the lens 30\u00a0d after explosion. The result is sensitive to the dark matter fraction in the lens: the solid blue and dashed orange lines correspond to the dark matter fractions derived in Auger et\u00a0al. (2010) for a Salpeter and Chabrier IMF, respectively.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig7.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/118349795/sty1346fig7.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig7.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"118349795\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/sty1346fig7.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&Signature=0r72-R3ZzK-~BS2jtSSx1cZJYxFiGOkeMCyTkJhMrTVREHQjLILdHZZBcd552OvEFH3L~sU4uQeecieKT1ddO1qnr6~YQ-tSKi8Kg4AlFVSS~9a-d-oxfhXAhIXtl-6ZbWb61yIXKRHnz5fVxBIHjfM1wTZSteXXV1JOL2FfTRMoRP19O2zSNUzCEitanHkSp9HTVOKVi5XEjnyWE~l4nrL72VbLj~Y46DgjNU7Yk~SiBWDgYOF-g~pFncJEl3hMbVSwJEQHsd6qsTF2yvslDdpfR1mDptZ2phz5V2EyEjsJ1YiZlpedLp3~9N6TeCUvReaZpMQDKfCIy9YMv3kN4A__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=118349795&ar=5001437&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig7.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The percentage of the source plane that is standardizable as a function of the Einstein radius <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> of the lens 30\u00a0d after explosion. The result is sensitive to the dark matter fraction in the lens: the solid blue and dashed orange lines correspond to the dark matter fractions derived in Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) for a Salpeter and Chabrier IMF, respectively.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig7\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 7.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/m_sty1346fig7.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&amp;Signature=InsEoKiFaL78pA-VkmsgI~Z5ZzgYERCQ25~wI9l9MKOICdiT9HuS8u0dz35dXiwomcHAxruh2Ok5br19PSBK2xHwPnM2zyJaIMjVp9g0moEUCYpvRnghkweF53PKq0RcU50UhJyEfIE1LZlAxseN4anl7vXDtTrWTARLyUzHk~YGGuR9mLtu9eUIOc8w6bwyo5a5EDA2pYE49WMyQ7ctUCl2TaOsvDbcvTWhZ3G-nzomDkNN6-GcHe0dLV6eNzzf~3ligHTgrFjvjAPpJ6bLP2iiNSs8xeLqoWlxoz~id-cTp0HjS8vQojEwR1lIJG6CO1JqIb~OAPJsM-OowXhp7g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The percentage of the source plane that is standardizable as a function of the Einstein radius REin of the lens 30\u00a0d after explosion. The result is sensitive to the dark matter fraction in the lens: the solid blue and dashed orange lines correspond to the dark matter fractions derived in Auger et\u00a0al. (2010) for a Salpeter and Chabrier IMF, respectively.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig7.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/118349795/sty1346fig7.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig7.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"118349795\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/4/10.1093_mnras_sty1346/3/sty1346fig7.jpeg?Expires=1611465999&Signature=0r72-R3ZzK-~BS2jtSSx1cZJYxFiGOkeMCyTkJhMrTVREHQjLILdHZZBcd552OvEFH3L~sU4uQeecieKT1ddO1qnr6~YQ-tSKi8Kg4AlFVSS~9a-d-oxfhXAhIXtl-6ZbWb61yIXKRHnz5fVxBIHjfM1wTZSteXXV1JOL2FfTRMoRP19O2zSNUzCEitanHkSp9HTVOKVi5XEjnyWE~l4nrL72VbLj~Y46DgjNU7Yk~SiBWDgYOF-g~pFncJEl3hMbVSwJEQHsd6qsTF2yvslDdpfR1mDptZ2phz5V2EyEjsJ1YiZlpedLp3~9N6TeCUvReaZpMQDKfCIy9YMv3kN4A__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=118349795&ar=5001437&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1346fig7.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The percentage of the source plane that is standardizable as a function of the Einstein radius <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> of the lens 30\u00a0d after explosion. The result is sensitive to the dark matter fraction in the lens: the solid blue and dashed orange lines correspond to the dark matter fractions derived in Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) for a Salpeter and Chabrier IMF, respectively.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In principle, measuring the scatter for a sample of GLSNe Ia with <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> \u223c 0.5 arcsec will allow us to discriminate between IMFs in the lensing galaxy. If the Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) Salpeter fit is correct then no lensed SNe with <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> &lt; 0.5 arcsec should have a scatter of less than 0.15 mags whilst most lensed SNe will if the Chabrier fit is correct.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The above toy model neglects magnification bias. Whilst asymmetric configurations dominate the source plane, they are less highly magnified and therefore harder to detect than more symmetric configurations. To illustrate this we take the mock GLSNe catalogue of Goldstein et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>) and assess the standardizable fraction of systems where the brightest SN image reaches a peak apparent <em>i</em>-band magnitude of 22.15. This choice roughly approximates the LSST discovery threshold for GLSNe. We predict that 22\u00a0per\u2009cent of the \u223c 930 GLSNe Ia to be discovered by LSST will be standardizable, of which approximately 1 in 5 will be quads. The median time delay for a standardizable LSST GLSN Ia is 44\u00a0d, compared to 18\u00a0d for all LSST GLSN Ia. The median Einstein radius for a standardizable LSST GLSN Ia is 0.9 arcsec, compared to 0.7 arcsec for all LSST GLSN Ia. The catalogue spans a range of Einstein radii between 0.06 arcsec \u2264<em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> \u2264 2.54 arcsec.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=118349798 id=\"118349798\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-3>3.3 Lifting the mass-sheet degeneracy with LSST GLSNe Ia: predictions for <em>H</em><sub>0</sub></h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The fundamental gain of a GLSNe Ia over a standard time delay lens is the ability to test for the presence of systematic uncertainties in the lens model. Since lens models have typical errors of a few percent (Wong et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), a 0.15 mag uncertainty on the flux of a single lensed image will not provide statistically relevant improvement on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. Averaging over many lensed supernovae will be required to constrain <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> with interesting accuracy.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To investigate the expected constraints on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> from the final LSST GLSNe Ia sample, we draw 650 GLSNe Ia from our mock LSST catalogue. This is the number of GLSNe Ia forecast to be discovered by LSST early enough to measure reliable time delays (Goldstein et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>). Taking a typical 7\u00a0per\u2009cent error per system (Bonvin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) and scaling by root N, gives <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\sigma _{H_0} = 0.3{\\rm \\,per\\,cent}$|\u2060</span>, however, this neglects residual systematics from the mass-sheet degeneracy.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Given the individual magnification probability <em>P</em>(\u03bc) for each image of a GLSN, the expected PDF for the unlensed magnitude of the supernova is given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ6\" content-id=\"equ6\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}P(M_{\\mathrm{SN}})= \\prod _{\\forall i} P(\\mu _i). \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(6)</span></div>Adding this in quadrature to an intrinsic SN Ia scatter of 0.1 mag (Betoule et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>), gives the expected uncertainty on the macromodel magnifications for the lens.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Constraining the true macromodel magnifications gives constraints on the mass-sheet degeneracy parameter \u03bb, since <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ7\" content-id=\"equ7\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\">\\begin{eqnarray*}\\mu _\\mathrm{True} = \\mu _\\mathrm{Model}/\\lambda ^2, \\end{eqnarray*}</div></div><span class=\"label\">(7)</span></div>where \u03bc<sub>True</sub> is the true magnification and \u03bc<sub>Model</sub> is the macromodel magnification assuming \u03bb\u00a0=\u00a00. This implies that a system with a microlensing scatter of 0.15 mag gives a constraint on \u03bb with 17\u00a0per\u2009cent precision that is insensitive to the mass-sheet transformation.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The time delays \u2013 and hence 1/<em>H</em><sub>0</sub> \u2013 are proportional to \u03bb. The product of <em>P</em>(\u03bb) over all the systems gives the level at which the mass-sheet degeneracy can be broken for the final constraint on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. This product has a width of \u03c3<sub>\u03bb</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.5\u00a0per\u2009cent. Combined, the 650 LSST GLSNe Ia will therefore be able to detect systematics in <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> due to the mass-sheet transformation at the 0.5\u00a0per\u2009cent level. If we restrict the sample to the 140 GLSNe with a microlensing scatter of less than 0.15 mag, the constraints on <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> degrade to <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\sigma _{H_0} = 0.6{\\rm \\,per\\,cent}$|\u2060</span>. If only the 44 standardizable quad image systems are used, the constraints <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> degrade to <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\sigma _{H_0} = 1.1{\\rm \\,per\\,cent}$|\u2060</span>.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=118349804 id=\"118349804\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-4>3.4 iPTF16geu</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The recent observations of iPTF16geu, give us a first opportunity to test the analysis methods developed in this paper. Since the images have high magnification and form in regions of high-stellar density, we should not expect this system to be standardizable. The values of \u03ba and \u03b3 for each of the iPTF16geu images have been estimated from the macro lens model published in More et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). We use the same prescription as in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec3-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3.2</a> to infer the likely smooth matter fractions at the image locations (Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"118349806\" scrollto-destination=\"118349806\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl1\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table 1.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Table of parameters used for iPTF16geu simulations. \u03ba and \u03b3 were taken from More et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Values of the smooth matter fraction <em>s</em> were inferred from our lens model of iPTF16geu.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th>Image<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u03ba<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u03b3<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>s</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>A\u00a0</td><td>0.56\u00a0</td><td>0.56\u00a0</td><td>0.22\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>B\u00a0</td><td>0.43\u00a0</td><td>0.43\u00a0</td><td>0.23\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>C\u00a0</td><td>0.57\u00a0</td><td>0.56\u00a0</td><td>0.27\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>D\u00a0</td><td>0.46\u00a0</td><td>0.45\u00a0</td><td>0.23\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th>Image<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u03ba<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u03b3<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>s</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>A\u00a0</td><td>0.56\u00a0</td><td>0.56\u00a0</td><td>0.22\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>B\u00a0</td><td>0.43\u00a0</td><td>0.43\u00a0</td><td>0.23\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>C\u00a0</td><td>0.57\u00a0</td><td>0.56\u00a0</td><td>0.27\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>D\u00a0</td><td>0.46\u00a0</td><td>0.45\u00a0</td><td>0.23\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;
